            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                        preloadLink.href = src;
                        preloadLink.as = "font";
                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

            createLink("/static/fonts/HardingText-Regular-Web.b167e675dc.woff2");
        </script>

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2013 497:7451"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-05-22"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Conifers have dominated forests for more than 200&#8201;million years and are of huge ecological and economic importance. Here we present the draft assembly of the 20-gigabase genome of Norway spruce (Picea abies), the first available for any gymnosperm. The number of well-supported genes (28,354) is similar to the &gt;100 times smaller genome of Arabidopsis thaliana, and there is no evidence of a recent whole-genome duplication in the gymnosperm lineage. Instead, the large genome size seems to result from the slow and steady accumulation of a diverse set of long-terminal repeat transposable elements, possibly owing to the lack of an efficient elimination mechanism. Comparative sequencing of Pinus sylvestris, Abies sibirica, Juniperus communis, Taxus baccata and Gnetum gnemon reveals that the transposable element diversity is shared among extant conifers. Expression of 24-nucleotide small RNAs, previously implicated in transposable element silencing, is tissue-specific and much lower than in other plants. We further identify numerous long (&gt;10,000&#8201;base pairs) introns, gene-like fragments, uncharacterized long non-coding RNAs and short RNAs. This opens up new genomic avenues for conifer forestry and breeding. The draft genome of the Norway spruce (P. abies) is presented; this is the first gymnosperm genome to be sequenced and reveals a large genome size (20 Gb) resulting from the accumulation of transposable elements, and comparative sequencing of five other gymnosperm genomes provides insights into conifer genome evolution. The first draft gymnosperm genome, that of a Norway spruce (Picea abies), is published this week by the Spruce Genome Project consortium. The genome is from a tree originally collected in 1959 in eastern J&#228;mtland, central Sweden. At 20 gigabases, the genome is more than a hundred times larger than that of the model plant species Arabidopsis, but the two contain a similar number of genes. The large genome size is the result of an accumulation of transposable elements. Comparative sequencing of five further gymnosperm genomes suggests that transposable element diversity is shared among extant conifers. The sequence data are available for public access from the ConGenIE website (
                  http://congenie.org/
                  
                )."/>

        <ul class="c-article-subject-list">
            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-sciences" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant sciences</a></li>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Conifers have dominated forests for more than 200 million years and are of huge ecological and economic importance. Here we present the draft assembly of the 20-gigabase genome of Norway spruce (<i>Picea abies</i>), the first available for any gymnosperm. The number of well-supported genes (28,354) is similar to the &gt;100 times smaller genome of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, and there is no evidence of a recent whole-genome duplication in the gymnosperm lineage. Instead, the large genome size seems to result from the slow and steady accumulation of a diverse set of long-terminal repeat transposable elements, possibly owing to the lack of an efficient elimination mechanism. Comparative sequencing of <i>Pinus sylvestris</i>, <i>Abies sibirica</i>, <i>Juniperus communis</i>, <i>Taxus baccata</i> and <i>Gnetum gnemon</i> reveals that the transposable element diversity is shared among extant conifers. Expression of 24-nucleotide small RNAs, previously implicated in transposable element silencing, is tissue-specific and much lower than in other plants. We further identify numerous long (&gt;10,000 base pairs) introns, gene-like fragments, uncharacterized long non-coding RNAs and short RNAs. This opens up new genomic avenues for conifer forestry and breeding.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Gymnosperms are a group of land plants comprising the extant taxa, cycads, <i>Ginkgo</i>, gnetophytes and conifers. Gymnosperms first appeared more than 300 million years ago (Myr ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Stewart, W. N. &amp; Rothwell, G. W. Paleobotany and the Evolution of Plants (Cambridge Univ. Press, 1993)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1389">1</a></sup>, well before the angiosperm lineage separated from the stem group of extant gymnosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Savard, L. et al. Chloroplast and nuclear gene sequences indicate late Pennsylvanian time for the last common ancestor of extant seed plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 91, 5163–5167 (1994)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1393">2</a></sup>. The major radiation of conifer families occurred 250–65 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leslie, A. B. et al. Hemisphere-scale differences in conifer evolutionary dynamics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 16217–16221 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1397">3</a></sup>, and during their evolution the morphology of conifers has changed relatively little. There are approximately 630 conifer species, representing about 70 currently recognized genera, which dominate many terrestrial ecosystems, especially in the Northern Hemisphere. Conifers also dominated both before and after the major mass extinction events at the end of the Permian and Cretaceous periods, around 250 and 65 Myr ago, respectively. Conifers are of immense ecological and economic value; coniferous forests cover enormous areas in the Northern Hemisphere, and conifers are keystone species in many other ecosystems. Conifers contribute a large fraction of terrestrial photosynthesis and biomass, and the cultural and economic values of conifers are also paramount; early civilizations used conifers for firewood, tools and artefacts and today several national economies depend on commodities produced from conifers. However, despite their abundance and importance, our understanding of conifer genomes is limited. Most conifers have 12 (2<i>n</i> = 24) chromosomes, probably reflecting the ancestral karyotype<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Flory, W. S. Chromosome numbers and phylogeny in the gymnosperms. J. Arnold Arb. 17, 82–87 (1936)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1405">4</a></sup>, which are typically of similar size, each being roughly comparable to the size of the human genome, and containing high proportions of repetitive elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Morse, A. M. et al. Evolution of genome size and complexity in Pinus. PLoS ONE 4, e4332 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1409">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kovach, A. et al. The Pinus taeda genome is characterized by diverse and highly diverged repetitive sequences. BMC Genomics 11, 420 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1412">6</a></sup>. The gene space of conifer genomes has not been well characterized, although several reports have suggested that gene families in conifers may be larger than their angiosperm counterparts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ahuja, M. R. &amp; Neale, D. B. Evolution of genome size in conifers. Silvae Genet. 54, 126–137 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1416">7</a></sup> and that conifer genomes contain numerous pseudogenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Buschiazzo, E., Ritland, C. E., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Ritland, K. Slow but not low: genomic comparisons reveal slower evolutionary rate and higher dN/dS in conifers compared to angiosperms. BMC Evol. Biol. 12, 8 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1420">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Because their genomes are among the largest—typically 20–30 gigabases pairs (Gb)—of all organisms, genome-wide analyses of conifers are particularly challenging. Thus, no full genome sequence of a gymnosperm species is available at present, whereas 30 angiosperm and more basal plant genomes have been sequenced. However, size is not the only challenge to sequencing presented by conifer genomes. Conifers are typically outbreeding, produce wind-dispersed pollen, have very large effective population sizes, and their genomes are highly heterozygous, although their nucleotide substitution rates are lower than those of most angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Buschiazzo, E., Ritland, C. E., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Ritland, K. Slow but not low: genomic comparisons reveal slower evolutionary rate and higher dN/dS in conifers compared to angiosperms. BMC Evol. Biol. 12, 8 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1427">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Jaramillo-Correa, J. P., Verdu, M. &amp; González-Martínez, S. C. The contribution of recombination to heterozygosity differs among plant evolutionary lineages and life-forms. BMC Evol. Biol. 10, 22 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1430">9</a></sup>, perhaps owing to long lifespan (decades to centuries). Furthermore, inbreeding depression negates the production of inbred lines that could facilitate genome assembly.</p><p>The availability of conifer genome sequences would enable comparative analyses of genome architecture and the evolution of key traits for seed plants, including flower or fruit development and life history (perennial versus annual), and help to determine how and why conifer genomes became so large. To address these issues and aid forest tree breeding, biodiversity and conservation studies by, for example, enabling the genome-wide design of genetic markers, we used data from massively parallel DNA sequencing to assemble a draft of the 20-Gb nuclear genome of Norway spruce (<i>Picea abies</i> (L.) Karst), one of the most widespread, ecologically and economically important plants in Europe. We analysed the protein-coding and non-coding fractions of the genome and compared them to the low-coverage draft genome assemblies of five other gymnosperms—Scots pine (<i>P. sylvestris</i>), Siberian fir (<i>A. sibirica</i>), juniper (<i>J. communis</i>), yew (<i>Taxus baccata</i>) and <i>Gnetum gnemon</i>—to gain insight into conifer genome evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Sequencing and assembly"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Sequencing and assembly</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We sequenced a 43-year-old root-grafted copy of the <i>P. abies</i> clone Z4006, which originated from a tree in Ragunda, central Sweden, collected in 1959. Many copies of this clone are available in clone archives and seed orchards, and it has been extensively used in Swedish breeding programs. We estimated its genome size to be 19.6 Gb (C = 20.02 ± 0.95 pg (mean ± s.d.); <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.1</a>), in accordance with previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Murray, B. G. Nuclear DNA amounts in gymnosperms. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 82, 3–15 (1998)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1470">10</a></sup>.</p><p><i>De novo</i> sequencing and assembly of large, repeat-containing, heterozygous genomes remains challenging. To assemble the <i>P. abies</i> genome, we developed a hierarchical strategy combining fosmid pools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhang, G. et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature 490, 49–54 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1482">11</a></sup> with both haploid and diploid whole genome shotgun (WGS) data, and RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq) data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vicedomini, R., Vezzi, F., Scalabrin, S., Arvestad, L. &amp; Policriti, A. GAM-NGS: genomic assemblies merger for next generation sequencing. BMC Bioinformatics 14, S6 (2013)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1486">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Sahlin, K., Street, N., Lundeberg, J. &amp; Arvestad, L. Improved gap size estimation for scaffolding algorithms. Bioinformatics 28, 2215–2222 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1489">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Vezzi, F., Narzisi, G. &amp; Mishra, B. Feature-by-feature–evaluating de novo sequence assembly. PLoS ONE 7, e31002 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1492">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.2–1.3</a>). The resulting assembly (P.abies 1.0) included 4.3 Gb in &gt;10-kilobase (kb) scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Tab1">Table 1</a>), and we estimated that approximately 63% of protein-coding genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ralph, S. G. et al. A conifer genomics resource of 200,000 spruce (Picea spp.) ESTs and 6,464 high-quality, sequence-finished full-length cDNAs for Sitka spruce (Picea sitchensis). BMC Genomics 9, 484 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1503">15</a></sup> were fully covered (&gt;90% of their length), and 96% partially covered (&gt;30% of their length) within single scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.4</a>). By mapping diploid reads to the P.abies 1.0 assembly, the single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) frequency was estimated to be 0.77% and the short insertion/deletion (indel) frequency to be 0.05% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Characteristics of the <i>P. abies</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature12211/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The chloroplast genome (124 kb) revealed considerable structural variation within the genus <i>Picea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.6</a>). The draft mitochondrial genome (&gt;4 Mb) was among the largest reported for plants and was rich in short open-reading frames (ORFs), which appeared to be gene remnants derived from repeat-driven mitochondrial rearrangements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Mackenzie, S. A. in Plant Mitochondria (ed. Logan, D. C. ) 36–49 (Blackwell, 2007)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1543">16</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.7</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Presence of long introns and gene-like fragments"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Presence of long introns and gene-like fragments</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>We generated &gt;1 billion RNA-Seq reads and used transcript assemblies of these in combination with public expressed sequence tags (ESTs) and transcripts to perform <i>ab initio</i> prediction of protein-coding genes, which identified a high confidence set of 28,354 loci with &gt;70% coverage by supporting evidence from the total set of 70,968 predicted loci. A notable characteristic of the predicted gene structures was the presence of numerous long introns (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>), with mean intron length being higher than in most available plant genomes, although similar to the repeat-rich genomes of <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and <i>Zea mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Messing, J. et al. Sequence composition and genome organization of maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 14349–14354 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1569">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1572">18</a></sup>. The longest intron in the high-confidence genes was 68 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Table 2.6</a>), and 2,384 high-confidence genes contained 2,880 longer than 5-kb introns (20 of which we confirmed by PCR amplification; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.14</a>), 2,679 of which contained a repeat, suggesting that repeat insertions account for intron expansion. By contrast, exon size was consistent among the species considered (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.6.3</a>). Numerous genes (<span class="stix">∼</span>30%) remained split across scaffolds owing to assembly fragmentation, and as such, the longest introns were not represented in the P.abies 1.0 assembly. Long introns (either individual or cumulative intron length) did not influence expression levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>) and introns containing repeats have not contracted despite a lack of recent repeat activity (see below).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The gene-space and transcribed fraction of the P.abies 1.0 assembly."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The gene-space and transcribed fraction of the P.abies 1.0 assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12211/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12211/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12211_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12211/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12211_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="528"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Gene family loss and gain in eight sequenced plant genomes (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Zea mays</i>, <i>Picea abies</i>, <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> and <i>Physcomitella patens</i>). Gene families were identified using TribeMCL (inflation value 4), and the DOLLOP program from the PHYLIP package was used to determine the minimum gene set for ancestral nodes of the phylogenetic tree. We used plant genome annotations filtered to remove transposable elements. ‘Orphans’ refers to gene families containing only a single gene. Blue numbers indicate the number of gene families. <b>b</b>, Boxplot representation of length distribution for the 10% longest introns in the same eight genomes. <b>c</b>, Scatter plots of cumulative intron length against log<sub>10</sub> expression calculated as fragments per kilobase per million mapped reads (FPKM) for high-confidence gene loci (top, coloured orange) and green for lncRNA loci (middle, shaded green). The bottom panel shows a histogram of cumulative intron size in the two sets of loci. <b>d</b>, Distribution of small (18–24-nucleotide (nt)) RNAs and their co-alignment-based colocation to genomic features (repeats, high-confidence genes and their promoter/UTRs). CDS, coding sequence.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM85">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12211/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Analysis of gene families in the high-confidence gene set and seven sequenced plant genomes (five angiosperms: <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> and <i>Zea mays</i>, and two basal plants: <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> and <i>Physcomitrella patens</i>) identified 1,021 <i>P. abies</i>-specific gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.8</a>). <i>P. abies</i>-specific families included over-representation of Gene Ontology categories involved in DNA repair and methylation of DNA and chromatin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.8</a>). As for most draft genomes, these results probably overestimate gene numbers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bennetzen, J. L., Coleman, C., Liu, R., Ma, J. &amp; Ramakrishna, W. Consistent over-estimation of gene number in complex plant genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 7, 732–736 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1695">19</a></sup> and will be refined as we further improve the genome assembly.</p><p>A common mechanism leading to genome size expansion is the occurrence of a whole genome duplication (WGD) event. We calculated the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) of paralogues within the high-confidence genes but found no evidence for any recent WGD; there was a clear, exponential decay in the number of retained paralogues with increasing <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Fig. 2.6</a>). However, a population dynamics model that takes into account both small- and large-scale modes of gene duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177–190 (2013)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1717">20</a></sup> suggested the presence of a small peak (around <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> of 1.1), which, considering the slow substitution rate of conifers, might represent the ancient WGD predating the divergence of angiosperms and gymnosperms (350 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1725">21</a></sup>).</p><p>Previous examinations of small genomic subsets indicated that conifer genomes contain numerous pseudogenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Morse, A. M. et al. Evolution of genome size and complexity in Pinus. PLoS ONE 4, e4332 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1732">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kovach, A. et al. The Pinus taeda genome is characterized by diverse and highly diverged repetitive sequences. BMC Genomics 11, 420 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1735">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="García-Gil, M. R. Evolutionary aspects of functional and pseudogene members of the phytochrome gene family in Scots pine. J. Mol. Evol. 67, 222–232 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1738">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Magbanua, Z. V. et al. Adventures in the enormous: a 1.8 million clone BAC library for the 21.7 Gb genome of loblolly pine. PLoS ONE 6, e16214 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1741">23</a></sup>. The gene-like fraction of the P.abies 1.0 assembly was identified by alignment of RNA-Seq reads and <i>de novo</i> assembled transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.10</a>). Within this subset of the genome, loci with valid spliced alignments of <i>de novo</i> assembled transcripts or the presence of a high-confidence gene were also identified. The high-confidence gene set represented 27 Mb of protein-coding sequence, whereas 72 Mb of regions were identified with a valid spliced alignment or a high-confidence gene. In stark contrast, 524 Mb of gene-like regions were identified by less stringent alignments. The presence of such a large gene-like fraction lacking predicted gene structures supports the presence of numerous pseudogenes.</p><p>Recent ENCODE publications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bánfai, B. et al. Long noncoding RNAs are rarely translated in two human cell lines. Genome Res. 22, 1646–1657 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1758">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Derrien, T. et al. The GENCODE v7 catalog of human long noncoding RNAs: analysis of their gene structure, evolution, and expression. Genome Res. 22, 1775–1789 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1761">25</a></sup> characterized numerous long non-coding RNA (lncRNA) loci in the human genome, but this class of RNA remains largely uncharacterized in plants. Using short-read <i>de novo</i> transcript assemblies, 13,031 spruce-specific and 9,686 conserved intergenic lncRNAs were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.4.3</a>). In common with the ENCODE results, <i>P. abies</i> lncRNA loci contained fewer exons, were shorter (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), and had more tissue-specific expression than protein-coding loci (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Fig. 2.8</a>).</p><p>There has been conflicting evidence about the presence of 24-nucleotide short RNAs (sRNAs) in gymnosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dolgosheina, E. V. et al. Conifers have a unique small RNA silencing signature. RNA 14, 1508–1515 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1784">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Morin, R. D. et al. Comparative analysis of the small RNA transcriptomes of Pinus contorta and Oryza sativa. Genome Res. 18, 571–584 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1787">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wan, L.-C. et al. Identification and characterization of small non-coding RNAs from Chinese fir by high throughput sequencing. BMC Plant Biol. 12, 146 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1790">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Zhang, J. et al. Dynamic expression of small RNA populations in larch (Larix leptolepis). Planta 237, 89–101 (2013)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1793">29</a></sup>, a class of sRNA that silence transposable elements by the establishment of DNA methylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Henderson, I. R. &amp; Jacobsen, S. E. Epigenetic inheritance in plants. Nature 447, 418–424 (2007)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1797">30</a></sup>. Across 22 samples, we identified numerous 24-nucleotide sRNAs, but these were highly specific to reproductive tissues, largely associated with repeats but present at substantially lower levels than in angiosperms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Fig. 2.10</a>). By contrast, 21-nucleotide sRNAs were associated with genes, repeats and promoters/untranslated regions (UTRs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>). <i>De novo</i> microRNA (miRNA) prediction identified 2,719 loci, including 20 known miRNA families, with target sites predicted within the high-confidence gene set for 1,378 of these (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.13</a>). Furthermore, 55 known miRNA families had &gt;5 aligned sRNA reads and mature miRNAs, representing 49 known families aligned to the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.13</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Conifer genomes grew by insertion of LTR-RT elements"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Conifer genomes grew by insertion of LTR-RT elements</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>We constructed a manually curated library of 1,773 repetitive sequences, approximately half of which could be assigned to known transposable element repeat families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 3.1–3.3</a>). Long terminal repeat-retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) comprised the most abundant fraction of transposable elements, with the <i>Ty3</i>/<i>Gypsy</i> superfamily being more abundant than the <i>Ty1</i>/<i>Copia</i> superfamily (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Tab1">Table 1</a>). We also identified and characterized transposable elements using 454 reads from randomly sheared genomic DNA in five other gymnosperms (<i>P. sylvestris</i>, <i>A. sibirica</i>, <i>J. communis</i>, <i>T. baccata</i> and <i>G. gnemon</i>) and, in all six species, LTR-RTs were the most abundant class (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 4.1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Table 3.1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Conifer genomes contain expansions of a diverse set of LTR-RTs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Conifer genomes contain expansions of a diverse set of LTR-RTs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12211/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12211/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12211_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12211/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12211_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1413"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of different classes of transposable elements from six gymnosperm species. The figure is based on the total fraction of transposable elements (TE) identified and grouped into different classes from the different species. Genome sizes of the six species are given in circles and their phylogenetic relationship is shown, with tentative dating of divergence times (<i>x</i>-axis) based on 64 chloroplast genes over 39 species and five fossil calibration points. <b>b</b>, <b>c</b>, Heuristic neighbour-joining trees constructed from 5,922 sequences similar to the <i>Ty3/Gypsy</i> (<b>b</b>) and 3,052 sequences similar to the <i>Ty1/Copia</i> (<b>c</b>) reverse transcriptase domain from nine plant species. The trees to the right have only sequences from <i>P. abies</i> and <i>Z. mays</i> coloured, whereas the grey dots are the uncoloured versions of the other species represented on the left. <b>d</b>, Distributions of insertion times calculated for LTR-RTs in <i>Picea abies</i>, <i>Picea glauca</i> and <i>Oryza glaberrima/O. sativa</i>, using mutation rates (per base per year) of 2.2 × 10<sup>−9</sup> for the <i>Picea</i> spp. and 1.8 × 10<sup>−8</sup> for <i>O. glaberrima</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1940">50</a></sup>.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM86">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12211/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To trace the history of transposable elements in vascular plants we inferred phylogenies of a domain of the reverse transcriptase genes of both <i>Ty1/Copia</i> and <i>Ty</i>3/<i>Gypsy</i> elements. The phylogenies revealed several diverse and ancient transposable element subfamilies, present in almost all of the examined conifer genera, whereas only a few subfamilies were expanded in the angiosperm genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig2">Fig. 2b, c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 3.11</a>). Most internal clades with significant bootstrap support were consistently species-specific, indicating that most expansions of extant transposable element families occurred after divergence. Two species-specific amplification bursts were evident: a <i>Ty1/Copia</i> family in <i>J. communis</i> and a <i>Ty3/Gypsy</i> family in <i>T. baccata</i>. We used complete LTR-RTs from <i>P. abies</i> and <i>P. glauca</i> to investigate further the timing of conifer transposable element insertions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sanmiguel, P. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Evidence that a recent increase in maize genome size was caused by the massive amplification of intergene retrotransposons. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 82, 37–44 (1998)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2876e1996">31</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 3.4–3.8</a>). In contrast to a similar set of elements identified in <i>Oryza sativa</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>), we detected no evidence of recent activity (that is, less than 5 Myr ago) in <i>P. abies.</i> Instead, insertions seem to have occurred over several tens of millions of years (older insertions are more likely to escape detection). Analysis of 68 orthologous transposable element insertions in <i>P. abies</i> and <i>P. glauca</i> further supported this: 63 insertions apparently predated divergence, and only five occurred after the lineages separated 13–20 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 3.9</a>).</p><p>We clustered LTRs of complete elements to identify transposable element families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nature Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2028">32</a></sup>. More than 86% of the elements remained as singletons, indicating that LTR-RTs are quite divergent and that there are several low-abundance families. We searched three LTR-RT families for signatures of unequal intra-element recombination events in scaffolds &gt;50 kb and 20 complete fosmids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075–1079 (2002)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2032">33</a></sup>. For families ALISEI, 3K05 and 4D08_5 we identified 21, 22 and 39 complete elements, and four, five and no solo LTRs, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 3.10</a>). Although this data set is limited, the analysis suggested that LTR-RT-related sequences might be removed less frequently by unequal recombination than in other plant genomes. The ratio of solo-LTRs to complete elements in <i>P. abies</i> is <span class="stix">∼</span>1:9, whereas in <i>A. thaliana</i>, rice and barley it is 1:1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075–1079 (2002)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2045">33</a>), 0.6:1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Vitte, C. &amp; Panaud, O. Formation of solo-LTRs through unequal homologous recombination counterbalances amplifications of LTR retrotransposons in rice Oryza sativa L. Mol. Biol. Evol. 20, 528–540 (2003)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2048">34</a>) and 16:1 (for the abundant BARE 1 element<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Vicient, C. M. et al. Retrotransposon BARE-1 and its role in genome evolution in the genus Hordeum. Plant Cell 11, 1769–1784 (1999)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2052">35</a></sup>), respectively. Taken together, these findings indicated that the extant set of transposable elements in <i>P. abies</i> accumulated slowly over tens or hundreds of millions years, mainly by the insertion of LTR-RT elements with limited transposable element removal.</p><p>An analysis of introns across taxa provided further insight into the genome of the last common ancestor to the conifers. We identified orthologues of normal sized (50–300 bp) and long (1–20 kb) introns in spruce within draft genome assemblies of <i>P. sylvestris</i> and <i>G. gnemon</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 4.2</a>). Introns identified as orthologous to a long intron in <i>P. abies</i> were also atypically long (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#Fig3">Fig. 3a, b</a>), suggesting that intron expansions started early in the history of conifers.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Intron sizes are conserved among gymnosperms."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Intron sizes are conserved among gymnosperms.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12211/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12211/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12211_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12211/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12211_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="419"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b>, Intron size comparisons between <i>P. abies</i>, <i>P. sylvestris</i> (<b>a</b>) and <i>G. gnemon</i> (<b>b</b>), respectively. Orthologues of introns that were categorised as short (50–300 bp) or long (1–20 kb) in <i>P. abies</i> were identified in <i>P. sylvestris</i> and <i>G. gnemon</i>, and the corresponding intron size was scored.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM87">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12211/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="The evolution of important conifer traits"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">The evolution of important conifer traits</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>Two major differences between angiosperms and gymnosperms are their contrasting reproductive development and the development of water-conducting xylem cells. We therefore manually identified <i>P. abies</i> loci homologous to genes known to be centrally involved in these processes in angiosperms.</p><p>In angiosperms, homologues of the <i>A. thaliana</i> phosphatidylethanolamine-binding protein (PEBP) FLOWERING LOCUS T (FT) are key activators of flowering. It has been suggested that gymnosperms lack orthologues of <i>FT</i> genes, instead containing a group of <i>FT</i>/<i>TFL1</i>-like genes that probably act as flowering repressors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Karlgren, A. et al. Evolution of the PEBP gene family in plants: functional diversification in seed plant evolution. Plant Physiol. 156, 1967–1977 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2160">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Klintenäs, M., Pin, P. A., Benlloch, R., Ingvarsson, P. K. &amp; Nilsson, O. Analysis of conifer FLOWERING LOCUS T/TERMINAL FLOWER1-like genes provides evidence for dramatic biochemical evolution in the angiosperm FT lineage. New Phytol. 196, 1260–1273 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2163">37</a></sup>. We identified four putative <i>FT</i>/<i>TFL1</i>-like genes in the P.abies 1.0 genome that have not been previously described and confirmed that the genome does indeed lack <i>FT</i>-like genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 5.1</a>).</p><p>MADS-box genes are involved in controlling most aspects of angiosperm development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Gramzow, L. &amp; Theissen, G. A hitchhiker’s guide to the MADS world of plants. Genome Biol. 11, 214 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2183">38</a></sup>. A total of 278 sequences with clear homology to MADS boxes were identified in the P.abies 1.0 assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 5.2</a>), 41 of which had transcript support. Type I and II MADS-box genes are distinguished in plants. Only 5% of the identified MADS boxes were of type I (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Fig. 5.2</a>.), the lowest percentage of potential type I genes recorded in any plant genome. Type II MADS-box genes are subdivided into about a dozen ancient clades. We observed remarkable expansions in the <i>TM3</i>-like (or <i>SOC1</i>-like), <i>STMADS11</i>-like and <i>TM8</i>-like gene clades in <i>P. abies.</i> Because members of these gene clades are involved in vegetative development and phase changes such as the floral transition in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Smaczniak, C. et al. Developmental and evolutionary diversity of plant MADS-domain factors: insights from recent studies. Development 139, 3081–3098 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2209">39</a></sup>, we propose that the expansion of these gene clades has contributed to the evolution of developmental phase changes in gymnosperms.</p><p>The xylem tissue of most gymnosperms comprises a single water-transporting cell type, tracheids. By contrast, the xylem tissue of angiosperm species contains fibres, originating from tracheids that have to a large extent lost the capacity to conduct mass water flows, and vessels that have taken over the water-transport function in the stem. Formation of vessels is controlled by the <i>VASCULAR NAC DOMAIN</i> (<i>VND</i>) gene family, which has seven members in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kubo, M. et al. Transcription switches for protoxylem and metaxylem vessel formation. Genes Dev. 19, 1855–1860 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2224">40</a></sup>. We detected two <i>VND</i> genes in <i>P. abies</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 5.3</a>), suggesting that co-option and expansion of the <i>VND</i> gene family in vessel formation might have been important for angiosperm evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="A model for conifer genome evolution"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">A model for conifer genome evolution</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>We propose the following model of conifer genome evolution. After the lineage that led to angiosperms had branched off and the most recent common ancestor of extant conifers had been established, the 12 ancestral chromosomes expanded at a slow and steady rate due to the activity of a diverse set of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> LTR transposable elements that are largely shared among extant conifers. The expansion started early and, in contrast to angiosperms genomes in which this has been counteracted by efficient recombination mechanisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075–1079 (2002)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2256">33</a></sup> resulting in only smaller transposable element subsets remaining following recent bursts of activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: dynamics of retrotransposition-driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2260">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hawkins, J. S., Kim, H., Nason, J. D., Wing, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Differential lineage-specific amplification of transposable elements is responsible for genome size variation in Gossypium. Genome Res. 16, 1252–1261 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2263">42</a></sup>, these elements have remained in the genome. We propose that mechanisms for transposable element removal (for example by unequal recombination) have been less active in conifers than in most other organisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Bennetzen, J. L., Ma, J. &amp; Devos, K. M. Mechanisms of recent genome size variation in flowering plants. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 95, 127–132 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2267">43</a></sup>, and our data suggest that the insertion of transposable elements into genes gave rise to large introns, and (combined with other mechanisms) abundant pseudogenes. Each chromosome has grown to a similar size—perhaps limited by physical constraints on, for instance, chromosomal replication—with genes separated by large regions of transposable-element-rich, highly polymorphic non-protein-coding regions with low recombination frequencies. The gradual increase in size, the lack of WGDs and a predominately out-crossing mating system have probably also buffered conifer genomes against chromosomal rearrangements (WGD reduces sensitivity to aneuploidy), thereby maintaining synteny over large phylogenetic distances<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Pavy, N. et al. A spruce gene map infers ancient plant genome reshuffling and subsequent slow evolution in the gymnosperm lineage leading to extant conifers. BMC Biol. 10, 84 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2272">44</a></sup>.</p><p>Some angiospems, such as cereals, also have large genomes but it seems as if the “one way ticket towards genome obesity”<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Do plants have a one way ticket to genomic obesity? Plant Cell 9, 1509–1514 (1997)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2279">45</a></sup> that is barely recognizable in angiosperms prevails in conifers. The underlying mechanism remains unclear, but the low frequency of 24-nucleotide sRNAs, their role in methylation of repeats and their restriction to reproductive tissues may have influenced the process. However, considering the effect of methylation patterns on recombination rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J. A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2283">46</a></sup> and the fact that 24-nucleotide sRNAs trigger methylation, such low recombination frequencies would more likely result from hypermethylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Fedoroff, N. V. Presidential address. Transposable elements, epigenetics, and genome evolution. Science 338, 758–767 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2287">47</a></sup>. A state of ‘genome paralysis’ could potentially have been triggered once an obesity threshold was reached. In the angiosperm lineage, the occurrence of a number of WGDs probably increased diversification potential, allowing morphological innovation (for example, the origin of flowers and fruits) and facilitating speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J. A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2291">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Van de Peer, Y. A mystery unveiled. Genome Biol. 12, 113 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2294">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Soltis, D. E. et al. Polyploidy and angiosperm diversification. Am. J. Bot. 96, 336–348 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2297">49</a></sup>. By contrast, the conserved genome structure resulting from the paucity of genome rearrangements and lack of WGDs in conifers probably limited the evolution of reproductive barriers (resulting in relatively low rates of speciation), and may explain the high degrees of conservation through time and low morphological diversity. Nevertheless, these processes do not seem to affect fitness as conifers dominate many ecosystems, probably because they contain high degrees of standing genetic variation, allowing them to occupy very wide ecological niches in climatic regions where other plant species are less competitive. The future availability of additional gymnosperm genome sequences will allow further exploration of the unique processes that have driven their evolution and facilitate improvement of this important species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>We shotgun-sequenced 450 fosmid pools containing around 100–6,000 fosmids per pool (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Table 1.4</a>). Each fosmid pool was assembled and scaffolded individually. Fosmid pool scaffolds larger than 1 kb (<span class="stix">∼</span>6.7 Gb in total) were merged<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vicedomini, R., Vezzi, F., Scalabrin, S., Arvestad, L. &amp; Policriti, A. GAM-NGS: genomic assemblies merger for next generation sequencing. BMC Bioinformatics 14, S6 (2013)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2312">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.3</a>) with a 38× haploid WGS assembly (<span class="stix">∼</span>9.8 Gb in total, derived from <span class="stix">∼</span>600 ng of DNA extracted from a single megagametophyte). We subsequently performed scaffolding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Sahlin, K., Street, N., Lundeberg, J. &amp; Arvestad, L. Improved gap size estimation for scaffolding algorithms. Bioinformatics 28, 2215–2222 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12211#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2876e2319">13</a></sup> using WGS libraries of five different insert sizes (0.3, 0.65, 2.4, 4.4 and 10.4 kb) from diploid tissue. We further increased assembly contiguity of protein-coding regions by scaffolding using a set of <span class="stix">∼</span>38 million unassembled (after digital normalization) RNA-Seq read-pairs generated from 22 samples (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 1.3</a>).</p><p><i>Ab initio</i> prediction of protein-coding genes was performed using ESTs from numerous conifer species, our own short-read <i>de novo</i> transcript assemblies and proteins from other plant species as supporting evidence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 2.6</a>). Predicted loci were used to perform gene family analysis and to examine the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> substitution rates of paralogues to identify evidence for a recent WGD event. <i>De novo</i> transcript assemblies were used to identify lncRNA, and sRNA sequencing was performed and used for <i>de novo</i> miRNA prediction.</p><p>Repeated sequences were identified <i>de novo</i> using 454 reads longer than 700 bp generated from randomly sheared genomic DNA. Candidates were characterized using similarity searches at the nucleotide and amino acid level against public and custom collections of plant transposable element sequences. Complete LTR-RTs were identified using a combination of <i>de novo</i> searches and manual inspection.</p><p>WGS assemblies from shallow sequencing (3.8–12.5×) of <i>P. sylvestris</i>, <i>A. sibirica</i>, <i>J. communis</i>, <i>T. baccata</i> and <i>G. gnemon</i> were produced using the CLC Bio <i>de novo</i> assembler.</p><p>For website and accession number information, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 6</a>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="accession-codes" data-title="Accession codes"><div class="c-article-section" id="accession-codes-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="accession-codes">Accession codes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="accession-codes-content">
              
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Data deposits</h3>
                <p>Raw data and assemblies are available from the ConGenIE (Conifer Genome Integrative Explorer) web resource (<a href="http://congenie.org">http://congenie.org</a>), as well as the European Bioinformatics Institute (EMBL) and European Nucleotide Archive (ENA); see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12211#MOESM88">Supplementary Information 6.1</a> for accession numbers.</p>

                    Google Scholar</a> 
                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="E. F.V., A.A., N.D., R.V., K.S. and E.S. contributed to the assembly and sequence analysis; S.G. to repeat analysis; N.D., M.E., L.G., M.Ku., T.N., Å.O., G.T., H.T., P.Z. and B.Z. to quality control of the assembly and analysis of gene families; K.H., J.H., O.K., M.Kä. and T.S. to the sequencing; L.K. to analysis of the mitochindrial genome; M.Ko. and N.R. to generation of fosmid pools; J.Lut., F.L., C.T.-L. and K.V. to analysis of the sequences of <i>P. abies</i> and other conifers; C.R. and J.S. to production of BAC sequences; Z.-Q.W. to analysis of the chloroplast genome and J.A.R. determined the genome size. L.A., R.B., J.Boh., J.Bou., R.G.G., T.R.H., P.d.J., J.M., M.M., K.R., B.S., S.L.T., Y.V.d.P. and B.A. contributed to the design and supervision of various parts of the research. O.N. headed and P.K.I. managed the project, J.Lun. coordinated the sequencing and assembly activities, and S.J. the bioinformatics activities. B.N., N.R.S., A.Z., O.N., P.K.I., J.Lun. and S.J. wrote and edited most of the manuscript. All authors commented on the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Conifer genome looms large</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The first draft gymnosperm genome, that of a Norway spruce (<i>Picea abies</i>), is published this week by the Spruce Genome Project consortium. The genome is from a tree originally collected in 1959 in eastern Jämtland, central Sweden. At 20 gigabases, the genome is more than a hundred times larger than that of the model plant species <i>Arabidopsis</i>, but the two contain a similar number of genes. The large genome size is the result of an accumulation of transposable elements. Comparative sequencing of five further gymnosperm genomes suggests that transposable element diversity is shared among extant conifers. The sequence data are available for public access from the ConGenIE website (<a href="http://congenie.org/">http://congenie.org/</a>).</p></div></section></div></aside>

                        </li>
                    
                </ul>
